<div id="review_1711786567" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1711786567" />
    <a title="S̶e̶a̶n̶" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40012758-s-e-a-n"><img alt="S̶e̶a̶n̶" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566235680p2/40012758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1711786567?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="S̶e̶a̶n̶" class="user" itemprop="url" name="S̶e̶a̶n̶" href="/user/show/40012758-s-e-a-n">S̶e̶a̶n̶</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394572536" href="/book/show/766672.Extinction">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40012758-s-e-a-n?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1711786567" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15882212682692925784"><br/>Goodreads certainly does not need another review of a Thomas Bernhard novel. However, having now completed my reading of his novels, I will shamelessly debase myself by dribbling out a few meager comments. This book was not what I expected, though going into it my expectations were admittedly vague. I figured it would present itself as Bernhard’s masterwork. Perhaps it is that, and I am unable to see it. My current disinterest in fiction smears any perspective beyond recognition. There are a few</span>
  <span id="freeText15882212682692925784" style="display:none"><br />Goodreads certainly does not need another review of a Thomas Bernhard novel. However, having now completed my reading of his novels, I will shamelessly debase myself by dribbling out a few meager comments. This book was not what I expected, though going into it my expectations were admittedly vague. I figured it would present itself as Bernhard’s masterwork. Perhaps it is that, and I am unable to see it. My current disinterest in fiction smears any perspective beyond recognition. There are a few elements of metafiction to the work that, while infrequently summoned, do hint at a certain <i>stepping back and surveying his life’s work</i> type of writerly affectation. Other than that this is a straightforward Bernhard novel. This time his narrator skewers familial relations in the wake of an accident that killed his parents and brother. Returning to his despised family estate in rural Austria from the self-imposed exile he leads in Rome, he immediately falls into an agitated state in which he abandons all decorum in favor of brooding on common Bernhardian concerns from various points of unseen observation located around the estate. If you’ve read Bernhard much of this will likely sound familiar. By now my reading of one of his novels has become rote. The lack of paragraph breaks is no longer a hindrance; my reading eye finds natural points at which to stop and pick back up without having to reread to find my place. And yet I almost quit this one several times out of pure disinterest. It took me a month to finish. Ultimately, though, the hypnotic sway of Bernhard’s prose did carry me along to the conclusion, which was quite satisfactory for a Bernhardian ending. While I’m glad I finished the book, I’m undecided as to whether I was in the wrong frame of mind to read it or if I simply have extracted all I needed from Bernhard and am finally able to move on. He will always remain an important writer to me for many reasons, but my anticipation that I would feel sorrow over completing his novels did not bear out. Instead I feel a sense of relieved release. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15882212682692925784" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2078632455" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12895785679010974097"><b>... i mille volti della scrittura...</b><br/><br/>Il protagonista abita a Roma, lontano dall'Austria, terra natìa.<br/>Arriva un telegramma: i genitori ed il fratello sono morti in un incidente. <br/>Dovrebbe esserci disperazione, tristezza, ma non è così. Il telegramma diventa, invece, motivo per sviscerare rabbia ed odio. <br/>Nulla ha scampo: la famiglia in primis è dichiarata morta. <br/>Le convenzioni sono carta straccia da bruciare. <br/>L'attaccamento ai soldi, la "recita" del lavoro, il lusso non condiviso (nella tenuta di fa</span>
  <span id="freeText12895785679010974097" style="display:none"><b>... i mille volti della scrittura...</b><br /><br />Il protagonista abita a Roma, lontano dall'Austria, terra natìa.<br />Arriva un telegramma: i genitori ed il fratello sono morti in un incidente. <br />Dovrebbe esserci disperazione, tristezza, ma non è così. Il telegramma diventa, invece, motivo per sviscerare rabbia ed odio. <br />Nulla ha scampo: la famiglia in primis è dichiarata morta. <br />Le convenzioni sono carta straccia da bruciare. <br />L'attaccamento ai soldi, la "recita" del lavoro, il lusso non condiviso (nella tenuta di famiglia ci sono ben 5 biblioteche che rimangono chiuse a chiave!!): non meritano che disprezzo.<br />Wolfseg (il villaggio dove è nato) è il fulcro dello spregio. <br />Ma è tutta la patria intera ad essere denigrata: la società e la cultura mitteleuropea sono da bruciare (<i>"perchè i mitteleuropei si comportano come marionette"</i>).<br />Il romanzo è quindi una sorta di lungo monologo dove ricordare non serve a trattenere il passato racchiuso in un’immagine, un odore, un colore (si riferisce a Proust??) ma, al contrario, <b>la rievocazione ha la funzione di estinguere ciò che è stato come se le parole producessero un fuoco catartico</b>. <br />La scrittura stessa diventa mezzo per fare tutto a pezzetti: uno sfacelo!!<br />Un ottimo romanzo per riflettere. <br />Sconsigliato a chi cerca l'azione.<br />( e con questo è nato il mio amore per Bernhard)<br /></span>

<div id="review_2473097141" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2473097141" />
    <a title="Niki Sven" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14255836-niki-sven"><img alt="Niki Sven" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591693030p2/14255836.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2473097141?book_show_action=true">Aug 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Niki Sven" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Niki Sven" href="/user/show/14255836-niki-sven">Niki Sven</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788679580733" href="/book/show/23765771-brisanje-raspad">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14255836-niki-sven?shelf=all-time-favs">all-time-favs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2473097141" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13610092852993124689">Jedan ogroman, 478 stranica dug pasus, u kome je sumiran sav duševni teret pisca - malograđanska porodica od koje se oduvek razlikovao i koja ga je uvek odbacivala, zatucan i hladan austrijski mentalitet, gađenje prema nacionalsocijalizmu i katolicizmu kao uništiteljima slobodnog duha, dekadencija čovečanstva i njegova neizvesna budućnost. Kroz ono što Bernhard naziva "umetnošću preterivanja", dočarava nam jedan mračni svet u kome čovek od duha nema izbora osim samo da proba da se od njega izbav</span>
  <span id="freeText13610092852993124689" style="display:none">Jedan ogroman, 478 stranica dug pasus, u kome je sumiran sav duševni teret pisca - malograđanska porodica od koje se oduvek razlikovao i koja ga je uvek odbacivala, zatucan i hladan austrijski mentalitet, gađenje prema nacionalsocijalizmu i katolicizmu kao uništiteljima slobodnog duha, dekadencija čovečanstva i njegova neizvesna budućnost. Kroz ono što Bernhard naziva "umetnošću preterivanja", dočarava nam jedan mračni svet u kome čovek od duha nema izbora osim samo da proba da se od njega izbavi ili pak da pusti da propadne. Služi se pritom brojnim ponavljanjima kako bi pojačao utisak i gotovo neprimetno skače s teme na temu kako bi omogućio radnji romana da se odvija. Preporuka je pročitati delo u originalu, jer je na osnovu prevodiočevih napomena jasno da se Bernhard služi igrom reči, koje u prevodu na srpski gube svoje prvobitno i jedinstveno značenje koje verovatno podosta doprinosi stilu pisanja kada je delo čitano na nemačkom. Uprkos svojoj mračnoj tematici, delo je beskrajno zabavno, naročito svakome ko se i inače gnuša malograđanštine i niskoumlja, i ko se sa istim poprilično često susretao u životu. Knjiga za čistu peticu i, po mom mišljenju, za Nobela.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13610092852993124689" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3506251583" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3506251583" />
    <a title="Eℓℓis ♥" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/46838158-e-is"><img alt="Eℓℓis ♥" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605795697p2/46838158.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3506251583?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eℓℓis ♥" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eℓℓis ♥" href="/user/show/46838158-e-is">Eℓℓis ♥</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788845912665" href="/book/show/16155320-estinzione">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/46838158-e-is?shelf=romanzi-amo">romanzi-amo</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3506251583" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer589379879915621218">E' su una fitta e arsenica scrittura che l'autore imbastisce questo flusso di pensieri di oltre 400 pagine - attraverso la voce narrante di Murau - e se ne serve per svilire le fragilità di una famiglia dove ipocrisia ed egoismo regnano sovrane... ma non si ferma qui. L'<b>estinzione</b> menzionata nel titolo è <u>totale</u>: un'invettiva che colpisce con ferocia non solo la famiglia e la cittadina di Wolfsegg, ma si estende a tutta la società mitteleuropea coinvolgendo le istituzioni e, talvolta, è il protag</span>
  <span id="freeText589379879915621218" style="display:none">E' su una fitta e arsenica scrittura che l'autore imbastisce questo flusso di pensieri di oltre 400 pagine - attraverso la voce narrante di Murau - e se ne serve per svilire le fragilità di una famiglia dove ipocrisia ed egoismo regnano sovrane... ma non si ferma qui. L'<b>estinzione</b> menzionata nel titolo è <u>totale</u>: un'invettiva che colpisce con ferocia non solo la famiglia e la cittadina di Wolfsegg, ma si estende a tutta la società mitteleuropea coinvolgendo le istituzioni e, talvolta, è il protagonista stesso - in un moto di ammissione delle sue mancanze - ad auto-condannarsi. Degna di nota, tanto per il suo essere molto attuale quanto per l'incisività dello scritto in sé, la disgressione sul concetto di "fotografia" e dell'atto del fotografare in continuazione, mortificato in un mero mettere in mostra una felicità artificiale e non autentica. Anche in questo romanzo emerge la caratteristica prosa asfissiante e intessuta di ricorsività propria di Bernhard, con alcune virate sul grottesco che vanno ad alleggerire un po' la tensione venutasi a creare. Non è un libro fatto di momenti concitati e azione; si tratta piuttosto di un romanzo che è un'esperienza di lettura <i>faticosa</i> a causa del nichilismo di cui è intriso, ma che merita di essere affrontato proprio per le tante le riflessioni che scaturiscono durante la lettura e che attecchiscono, spingendoti a rielaborarle nel tempo.</span>
  <a data-text-id="589379879915621218" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2079754724" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2079754724" />
    <a title="Héctor Genta" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/69879260-h-ctor-genta"><img alt="Héctor Genta" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541341679p2/69879260.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2079754724?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Héctor Genta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Héctor Genta" href="/user/show/69879260-h-ctor-genta">Héctor Genta</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788845912665" href="/book/show/16155320-estinzione">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69879260-h-ctor-genta?shelf=austriaci">austriaci</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2079754724" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7931936486147960606">J'accuse bernhardiano contro tutto e tutti. Un libro che parla di morte e dell'incapacità (impossibilità?) di accettare lo status quo. Una lettura a tratti faticosa, un monologo torrenziale dove il protagonista, Franz Josef Murau/Thomas Bernhard, con una prosa ossessiva, fatta di reiterazioni continue quasi fosse lingua parlata, lancia i suoi strali di volta in volta contro la famiglia, l'Austria, la Chiesa, il nazionalsocialismo (ma anche il socialismo per come è stato applicato) e in genere co</span>
  <span id="freeText7931936486147960606" style="display:none">J'accuse bernhardiano contro tutto e tutti. Un libro che parla di morte e dell'incapacità (impossibilità?) di accettare lo status quo. Una lettura a tratti faticosa, un monologo torrenziale dove il protagonista, Franz Josef Murau/Thomas Bernhard, con una prosa ossessiva, fatta di reiterazioni continue quasi fosse lingua parlata, lancia i suoi strali di volta in volta contro la famiglia, l'Austria, la Chiesa, il nazionalsocialismo (ma anche il socialismo per come è stato applicato) e in genere contro tutto quello che gli capita a tiro (ce n'è anche per Goethe e la fotografia intesa come mistificazione della realtà). Il tutto per la volontà di affrancarsi da un mondo che il protagonista rifiuta ma dal quale si sente contagiato, per estinguere quello che è stato, le radici e i valori sui cui si fonda la società e nei quali non si riconosce. C'è pochissima azione in questo romanzo, quasi tutto quello che succede avviene “dentro” a Josef Franz. Le sue invettive, i suoi giudizi su persone e istituzioni, non sfociano in conflitto aperto ma rimangono compressi all'interno del suo animo come se anche lui fosse, in fondo, schiacciato dal mondo di convenzioni che vuole distruggere (emblematiche, a questo proposito, le pagine nelle quali corre a spalancare le finestre della dimora di Wolfsegg per lasciare entrare l'aria e la luce e quelli in cui apre le porte delle biblioteche di casa per togliere la polvere ai libri, per farli vivere. Almeno loro). Non condividendo gli ideali dei genitori e della società austriaca, Josef Franz prova a costruirsi una vita a Roma, lontano dall'ambiente familiare, ma le morti dei genitori e del fratello lo riporteranno a Wolfsegg, quasi a testimoniare che non è possibile estinguere il proprio passato semplicemente allontanandosi. Non si può fuggire, per estinguere è necessario tornare e fare i conti con le proprie radici in maniera definitiva. Non si tratterà di un'estinzione indolore, perché Murau/Bernhard è cosciente di essere stato contagiato dall'ambiente e dagli ideali che vuole combattere e sa perfettamente che la strada che ha deciso di percorrere è senza uscita. Non è possibile liberarsi da un mondo di cui si fa parte, distruggere tutto vuol dire distruggere anche se stessi, un vicolo cieco per una conclusione quasi musiliana: pensare significa fallire, agire significa fallire. Trovo Bernhard spiazzante, eccessivo. Non si ferma davanti a niente e a nessuno, non fa sconti ne concessioni e intinge la penna nel veleno non tanto per il gusto di provocare quanto per il bisogno di dire quello che sente. Aggiungerei che a me Bernhard non fa ridere per niente (come invece ha scritto J. Marias sostenendo che trovava le sue invettive “irresistibilmente e intenzionalmente divertenti”), magari è vero che la tragedia sfocia a volte nel grottesco, ma da qui a ridere... <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7931936486147960606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_350200296" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/350200296" />
    <a title="Stephen Durrant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1457001-stephen-durrant"><img alt="Stephen Durrant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362701086p2/1457001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/350200296?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen Durrant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen Durrant" href="/user/show/1457001-stephen-durrant">Stephen Durrant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer350200296" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8553416922379365099">No one else writes quite like Thomas Bernhard, certainly one of the most distinctive stylists of the second half of the twentieth-century. His novels typically are constructed of rants addressed by a first-person narrator to a silent listener. These rants are always exaggerated, as Franz-Josef Murau, the narrator of "Extinction" realizes: "Without the art of exaggeration, I told him, we'd be condemned to an awfully tedious life, a life not worth living. And I've developed this art to an incredib</span>
  <span id="freeText8553416922379365099" style="display:none">No one else writes quite like Thomas Bernhard, certainly one of the most distinctive stylists of the second half of the twentieth-century. His novels typically are constructed of rants addressed by a first-person narrator to a silent listener. These rants are always exaggerated, as Franz-Josef Murau, the narrator of "Extinction" realizes: "Without the art of exaggeration, I told him, we'd be condemned to an awfully tedious life, a life not worth living. And I've developed this art to an incredible pitch, I said" (p65). Murau turns his "art" upon motherhood (". . . most of them are perverse puppet mothers who tug at the heads of their families until they've tugged them to death"), Germany (a country that exemplifies "the tyranny of thee-ring binders"), the German language ("essentially an ugly language" -- but, of course, the language in which Bernhard is writing), Goethe ("a charlatan"), funerals ("pure theater"), photography ("a vulgar addiction"), doctors ("the most uninteresting people in the world) and many other subjects, but mostly upon Austrians ("by nature the Austrian is a National Socialist and a Catholic through and through, no matter how hard he tries not to be") and upon his hometown of Wolfsegg ("a citadel of brainlessness"). What is sometimes missed in Bernhard, however, is that all this anger directed so nastily, albeit often lucidly, at so many targets is a way of "concealing my own beastliness" (p237), and perhaps of "extinguishing everything" (p106) as a preface to one's own extinction. Bernhard's novels, it seems to me, are really about masking a horrible sense of isolation and impending doom with an outwardly directed fury. But what is so skillful about this particular novel is how much of a story takes shape in the interstices of these diatribes. Franz-Josef lives in Rome as an expatriate, funded largely it seems by his father's largesse. That father, along with his mother and his older brother, are killed in an automobile accident, and Franz-Josef must go back to the Austrian town he detests (and in some ways simultaneously loves) in order to preside over their funeral and somehow come to terms with his estranged sisters and the fact that he has now inherited the huge Austrian estate he claims to loath so much. This is a rewarding read, although Bernhard deals out the kind of medicine that only those of the strongest psychological constitutions should take in anything more than small doses.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8553416922379365099" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3488630" />
    <a title="Jeff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/218436-jeff"><img alt="Jeff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1195630966p2/218436.jpg" /></a>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8YE8qsUYoeKKZ+xri82zjLoC8x1OTNLAsmnIw86ZtYiENcdnCgDoqI4mvbUYL1XcaGK/sQwhYbJJq2Rl+t51iQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('uKj/tWcHJlx8gFQWHVZzcd1DT/jDa6Jb1pQ2DYG8mo3NHAR4qB9vFnjBBciOtJUhDyMDVIEGESktVpqrtftajA==')})
  }
//]]>

Largely self-taught and altogether indifferent to worldly success, Walser wrote a range of short stories, essays and four novels, of which Jakob von Gunten is widely recognized as the finest. It tells the story of a seventeen-year-old runaway from an old family who enrolls in a school for servants. The Institute, run by the domineering Herr Benjamenta and his beautiful but ailing sister, is a deeply mysterious place: the faculty lies asleep in a single room. The students though subject to fierce discipline, come and go at will. Jakob, an irrepressibly subversive presence, keeps a journal in which he records his quirky impressions of the school as well as his own quickly changing enthusiasms and uncertainties, deliberations and dreams. And in the end, as the Institute itself dissolves around him like a dream, he steps out boldly to explore still-unimagined worlds.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"16704266290447162380\" href=\"#\" No sooner has he arrived however, than he discovers that his ex-wife is pursuing him. He flees, she follows, and soon the couple is running circles around each other across the length of America—from Philadelphia to St. Louis to the Arizona desert, and from Portland, Oregon, to L.A. Is it love or vengeance that they want from each other? Everything’s spectacularly unclear in a book that is travelogue, suspense story, domestic comedy, and Western showdown, with a totally unexpected Hollywood twist at the end. Above all, Short Letter, Long Farewell is a love letter to America, its landscapes and popular culture, the invitation and the threat of its newness and wildness and emptiness, with the promise of a In it he captures the Isherwood-like atmosphere of Nazi Germany while telling a satiric story about the rise to power of one man - a thinly veiled caricature of his own brother-in-law. The man is Hendrik Hofgen, a character actor who in his own life plays a bizarre part in the elite circle of the Third Reich. Hofgen is publicly a revolutionary, but secretly he is a man driven by an obsessive need for power and fame. Although he benefits from the prestige of being married to the daughter of an eminent politician, he endangers his rise in Nazi society by his compulsive involvement with ‘a black Venus.’ His brilliant success as Mephisto in FAUST brings him the support of the Führer’s prime minister, who appoints him head of the State Theater. His dreams are finally realized, but the story ends on a note of despair as Hofgen is forced to confront the emptiness of his life. Mann weaves his tale with amazing skill. The result is a fascinating novel of decadence and evil.\n\nKlaus Mann, the second child of Thomas Mann, was born in Munich in 1906. He began writing short stories and articles in 1924, and within a year was a theatrical critic for a Berlin newspaper. In 1925 both a volume of short stories and his first novel, THE PIOUS DANCE, were published. His sister, Erika, to whom he was very close, was in the cast of his first play, ANJA AND ESTHER. Mann left Germany in 1933 and lived in Amsterdam until 1936, during which time he became a Czechoslovakian citizen, having been deprived of his German citizenship by the Nazis. He moved to America in 1936, living in Princeton, New Jersey, and New York City. He became a U.S. citizen in 1943. He died at the age of forty-two in Cannes, France. He was an idealistic loner who strove for an understanding of the hidden forces of both the world and the human mind; and he represented those forces in the most potent framework available to him—his stories of the supernatural.\n\nWith no one to champion him in his own land, it took until 1993 and the publication of The Dark Domain (translated by Miroslaw Lipinski) for English language readers to become truly aware of this undisputed master of original and interesting fantastic fiction.\n\nThe stories of The Motion Demon are peopled by memorable characters, and even a simple railway journey offers telling clues to the metaphysical and beyond. All aboard for a unique adventure!\n\nWith this first complete translation of The Motion Demon, Ash-Tree Press begins a comprehensive series of Grabinski volumes that will duplicate the content of the original Polish editions and also assemble short stories which never saw book publication. There is no concrete plot or setting - and whether the other characters (&quot;Mahood&quot; (formerly &quot;Basil&quot;) and &quot;Worm&quot;) actually exist or whether they are facets of the narrator himself is debatable. The protagonist also claims authorship of the main characters in the two previous novels of the Trilogy and Beckett\'s earlier novels Murphy, Mercier and Camier, and Watt. The novel is a mix of recollections and existential musings on the part of its narrator, many of which pertain specifically to the possibility that the narrator is constructed by the language he speaks. Other \'characters\' (a stretch to call them distinctly different than the narrator) serve as the passive recipient of the dialogue and in many places (as the narrator suggests) the dialogue\'s genesis. The novel builds in its despairing tone until the ending, which consists mainly of very long run-on sentences. It closes with the phrase &quot;I can\'t go on, I\'ll go on,&quot; which was later used as the title of an anthology of Beckett works.\n\nThe Unnamable is a 1953 novel by Samuel Beckett. It is the third and final entry in Beckett\'s &quot;Trilogy&quot; of novels, which begins with Molloy followed by Malone Dies. It was originally published in French as L\'Innommable and later translated by the author into English. <span id="freeTextContainer9401550515739232522">Thomas Bernhard was an Austrian author, who ranks among the most distinguished German speaking writers of the second half of the 20th century.<br/><br/>Although internationally he's most acclaimed because of his novels, he was also a prolific playwright. His characters were oftenly working in a lifetime and never-ending major work while they deal with themes such as suicide, madness and obsession and, as Be</span>
  <span id="freeText9401550515739232522" style="display:none">Thomas Bernhard was an Austrian author, who ranks among the most distinguished German speaking writers of the second half of the 20th century.<br /><br />Although internationally he's most acclaimed because of his novels, he was also a prolific playwright. His characters were oftenly working in a lifetime and never-ending major work while they deal with themes such as suicide, madness and obsession and, as Bernhard did, they use to have a love-hate relation with Austria. His prose was tumultuous but sober at the same time, philosophic in the background, with a musical cadency and plenty of black humor.<br /><br />He started publishing in the year 1963, with the title <b>"Frost"</b>. His last published work, appeared in the year 1986, was <b>"Extinction"</b>. Some of his most well known works include <b>"The loser"</b> (where he ficitionalizes about Glenn Gould), <b>"Correction"</b> and <b>"Woodcutters"</b>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9401550515739232522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/25436242-ausl-schung-ein-zerfall">Quotes from Extinction</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;We must allow ourselves to think, we must dare to think, even though we fail. It is in the nature of things that we always fail, because we suddenly find it impossible to order our thoughts, because the process of thinking requires us to consider every thought there is, every possible thought. Fundamentally we have always failed, like all the others, whoever they were, even the greatest minds. At some point, they suddenly failed and their system collapsed, as is proved by their writings, which we admire because they venture farthest into failure. To think is to fail, I thought.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/536625-we-must-allow-ourselves-to-think-we-must-dare-to">79 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;We have to keep company with supposedly bad characters if we are to survive and not succumb to mental atrophy. People of good character, so called, are the ones who end up boring us to death.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
